NATIONAL FLOOD INSURANCE PROGRAM

CLAIMS
MANUAL

<!-- Page 3 -->

As our trusted 
industry partners, 
claims professionals 
are often the first 
representatives of 
the NFIP to engage 
with our policyholders 
on the worst day of 
their lives following 
a flood event. That 
is why we rely on 
you to bring your expertise to every interaction, 
displaying FEMA’s core values of compassion, 
fairness, integrity, and respect.
The Federal Insurance Directorate is committed 
to reducing complexity for our policyholders 
and stakeholders. As part of the effort, we 
redesigned our NFIP Claims Manual so that 
it more clearly maps each step of the Claims 
Journey — from the time of loss through 
the resolution of the claim. It serves as a 
central source for both our customers and 
stakeholders, providing details and updates 
about the NFIP, the Standard Flood Insurance 
Policy, essential forms, coverage limits, claims 
processes, and much more, to ensure that you 
are equipped with the knowledge and tools to 
handle every claim with confidence. 
We are making additional changes to help 
individuals and communities better prepare 
and recover from flood. We are enhancing 
the message to include more risk education 
information and using underwriting factors 
to encourage risk discussions with property 
owners. Finally, we are working with 
communities to help them better understand 
and mitigate evolving flood risks.
These efforts are intended to further the 
Federal Insurance Directorate’s mission of 
providing customer-focused flood insurance to 
help survivors and their communities recover 
effectively from flood events. We know the 
secret to success is in the details. Paying 
attention to the details shows that we are 
invested in our policyholders’ recovery, and it 
allows us to reduce disaster suffering.
I have full confidence that you will continue the 
high-quality service that our NFIP customers 
have come to expect. I am grateful for your 
work to help policyholders recover from flood 
losses, and I look forward to your continued 
commitment as we work together to build a 
safer, more resilient nation. 
— Elizabeth Asche, Ph.D.
Assistant Administrator 
Federal Insurance Directorate 
Message to National Flood Insurance Program Claims Professionals

INTRODUCTION

---
<!-- Page 21 -->

NFIP Claims Manual  |  Introduction
MARCH 2025 EDITION	
INTRO – 1
The purpose of the NFIP Claims Manual (“Manual”) is to provide claims 
guidance to flood insurance claims professionals, which includes NFIP 
insurers, examiners, NFIP insurer’s staff adjusters, independent adjusters, and 
adjusting firms, so policyholders experience consistent and reliable service. 
The Manual contains processes for all aspects of the claims journey, from the 
notice of loss to final payment.
This Manual supersedes all prior NFIP flood insurance claims bulletins, except 
those announcing:
	
• Event Designation Numbers (EDNs), 
	
• Flood Response Office (FRO) locations, and
	
• Claims adjuster briefings.  
Purpose
Note: This document includes hyperlinks to websites and PDFs. These links are 
current as of the date of publication. Some links may perform better in different 
web browsers. Due to the interactive nature of some PDF forms, it may be 
necessary to download PDFs to your computer in order to open them in a PDF 
viewer. See https://www.fema.gov/flood-insurance/find-form/adjusters for further 
information on how to download and use the forms.
Disclaimer: This document represents the current FEMA guidance on the 
covered topics and may assist NFIP insurers, examiners, agents, adjusters, and 
policyholders to apply applicable statutory and regulatory requirements, as well as 
the terms and conditions of the Standard Flood Insurance Policy. This document 
is not a substitute for applicable legal requirements, nor is it itself a rule. It is not 
intended to, nor does it impose, legally binding requirements on any party, except 
where parties have voluntarily entered into an agreement requiring compliance 
with FEMA guidance. FEMA’s discussion of any brand, trademark, or registered 
mark is not an endorsement. References to states and communities throughout 
this Manual include NFIP-participating tribal governments.

---
<!-- Page 22 -->

INTRO – 2	
MARCH 2025 EDITION
Introduction  |  NFIP Claims Manual
Congress created the National Flood Insurance Program (NFIP) in 1968 
with a goal of making flood insurance available nationwide. It sought to 
accomplish this through effective partnership across all levels of government, 
the insurance industry, lending institutions, and property owners. FEMA 
administers the NFIP and is responsible for selling flood insurance; identifying 
and publishing flood hazard data; and establishing floodplain management 
criteria guiding construction in the floodplain.
To participate in the NFIP and be eligible to purchase flood insurance, 
communities must adopt and enforce land use ordinances that meet or exceed 
FEMA’s minimum floodplain management regulations and adopt the effective 
Flood Insurance Rate Map (FIRM). FEMA markets flood insurance in two ways: 
the NFIP Direct and the Write Your Own (WYO) Program, which enables eligible 
private insurance companies to sell and service NFIP policies under the 
company’s own name.
Note: The Standard Flood Insurance Policy (SFIP) forms and Group Flood 
Insurance Policy are under federal law, unlike other property lines.
National 
Flood 
Insurance 
Program
FEMA expects every flood insurance claims professional to follow the guidance 
in this Manual and be knowledgeable of the provisions, exclusions, and 
restrictions of the SFIPs. Flood insurance claims professionals should also 
determine whether an NFIP bulletin issued after the publication of this Manual 
gives policy and claim clarifications. NFIP Bulletins are located at https://
nfipservices.floodsmart.gov, under the Industry Resources link. 
Note: FEMA recommends adjusters and claims examiners subscribe to the 
WYO Clearinghouse Bulletin Subscription distribution list located at https://
nfipservices.floodsmart.gov/home/mailinglist. 
NFIP 
Knowledge
FEMA must establish the terms and conditions of flood insurance coverage 
by regulation. See 42 U.S.C. § 4013(a). FEMA accomplishes this through the 
Standard Flood Insurance Policy (SFIP) forms. See 44 C.F.R. § 61.13 (2024); 
44 C.F.R. Pt. 61, App. A(1)-(3) (2024). NFIP insurers must use the SFIP forms. 
The SFIP defines the coverages, limitations, and exclusions for NFIP flood 
insurance policies and includes terms and conditions that are unique to the 
NFIP. There are three different SFIP forms: the Dwelling Form, the General 
Property (GP) Form, and the Residential Condominium Building Association 
Policy (RCBAP) Form. See 44 C.F.R. Pt. 61, App. A(1)-(3) (2024).
See the NFIP Flood Insurance Manual for additional information about eligible 
policyholders and building occupancies for each SFIP.
Standard 
Flood 
Insurance 
Policy

---
<!-- Page 23 -->

NFIP Claims Manual  |  Introduction
MARCH 2025 EDITION	
INTRO – 3
TABLE 1: Standard Flood Insurance Policy Forms
SFIP Form
Eligible Policyholders
Eligible Building Occupancies
Dwelling Form

In order to adjust flood losses for the NFIP, independent adjusters must both 
register with the NFIP and possess an active Flood Control Number (FCN). 
To receive an FCN, an independent adjuster must possess the requisite 
qualifications and attend an annual NFIP claims presentation.
The FCNs are unique identifiers issued by FEMA that are assigned to each 
adjuster and adjusting firm, authorizing them to adjust specific types of NFIP 
claims. Adjusters must carry their FCN card at all times and present it to the 
policyholder upon request. 
Becoming 
an NFIP 
Adjuster
 FCN cards are FEMA property and may only be used by the person to whom it was 
issued, for the sole purpose of adjusting NFIP claims on behalf of the NFIP insurer.

Introduction  |  NFIP Claims Manual
Only licensed independent adjusters with active FCNs may adjust NFIP claims. 
The FCN is used to identify and authorize adjusters to handle claims for the 
NFIP. The FCN card confirms the adjuster is active, registered, and specifies 
the types of claims they are authorized to handle under the NFIP. NFIP insurers’ 
staff adjusters are not required to have an FCN, but must have the same 
specialized knowledge standards as those with an FCN.

INTRO – 5
During large or mid-sized events, FEMA may 
implement the Flood Adjuster Capacity Program 
to ensure there are sufficient qualified adjusters 
available to adjust claims. For more information, 
please see the Flood Adjuster Capacity Program 
Manual.
 Adjusters may be authorized to handle claims under 
the FACP and issued unique FCN cards, which are vertical 
in orientation and contain unique identifiers. These 
cards remain FEMA property and may only be used by 
the designated adjuster for the exclusive purpose of 
adjusting claims on behalf of the NFIP insurer. The card 
is valid only until the adjuster’s training is finalized.
To be eligible to obtain an FCN and adjust NFIP claims, a licensed independent 
adjuster must:
	
• Meet the requirements described in Table 2 of this section;
	
• Apply for and obtain an FCN;
	
• Attend annual FEMA-approved NFIP claims presentations and  
workshops; and
	
• Abide by the NFIP’s Code of Conduct and by the FEMA Core Values.
More details on how to apply for an FCN can be found at FloodSmart.gov. 
The complete Adjuster Registration Application must be sent to FEMA via the 
following method:
	
Email: NFIPAdjusterMailbox@fema.dhs.gov
FEMA will send the adjuster an approval or denial notification by email to new 
adjusters and adjusters seeking to add categories to their FCNs. If approved, 
FEMA will issue the adjuster an FCN card by email. FCN cards are considered 
FEMA property. FCN cards may only be used by the designated adjuster for 
the exclusive purpose of adjusting NFIP claims on behalf of the NFIP insurer. 
Unauthorized use of the FCN card is strictly prohibited. The assigned FCN will 
be active for one year. Provided the adjuster continues to attend annual FEMA-
approved NFIP claims presentations and abides by the Code of Conduct, FEMA 
will automatically renew the FCN card. Note: FEMA reserves the right to request 
a new application from any adjuster when it determines updates in information 
are needed. 
If an adjuster fails to attend the annual FEMA-approved NFIP claims 
presentation, the adjuster’s FCN will become inactive, and the adjuster will be 
unable to adjust NFIP claims. Adjusters with inactive FCNs will need to reapply 
for an FCN. FEMA maintains a database of active and inactive NFIP adjusters, 
and records of adjusters’ attendance at annual FEMA-approved NFIP claims 
presentations and workshops.

Introduction  |  NFIP Claims Manual
Flood Insurance 
Claims 
Professional 
Expectations
Adjusting 
Companies
Adjusting companies have a pivotal role in the oversight of independent 
adjusters handling claims under their company banner, to include training, 
knowledge of the NFIP policies and processes, and maintaining NFIP 
authorized adjusters. 
The adjusting company will work with the WYO companies and NFIP Direct for 
instructional guidance. The adjusting company should:
	
• Distribute claim assignments to the proper adjusters based on registration 
level and professional qualifications.
Adjusters
The NFIP insurer assigns the adjuster to help the 
policyholder document the loss and to make a 
recommendation on the claim to the insurer, which 
is shared with the policyholder. FEMA expects every 
adjuster handling NFIP flood losses to:
	
• Explain the policy to the policyholder and make 
recommendations to the NFIP insurer;
	
• Communicate to the policyholder that the 
adjuster does not have the authority to approve or deny a claim, only to 
make recommendations subject to review by the NFIP insurer;
	
• Adjust all claims in compliance with the SFIP, this Manual, and any 
applicable bulletin issued by FEMA after publication of this Manual; and
	
• Review and explain the building estimates and proactively assist 
policyholders with the contents claim and Proof of Loss (POL).
FEMA’s goal is to ensure that flood insurance claims professionals, including 
independent adjusters, adjusting firms, examiners, and NFIP insurers’ staff 
adjusters, handle each claim fairly, without unnecessary delay, and to see 
that everyone involved in the flood claim process treats each policyholder with 
respect and empathy.
	
• Adjusters will likely be the first, and perhaps the only, NFIP insurer’s 
representative a policyholder meets after a flood. The NFIP policyholders 
depend on the adjusters’ expertise and compassion to help them recover 
from what may be a devastating experience for them.
	
• NFIP insurers have the authority to examine claims, make coverage 
decisions, and communicate the claims determinations to policyholders. 
How the examiner handles their oversight of flood claims can positively 
influence the customer experience.
	
• Pay the policyholder as soon as possible, including all undisputed amounts. 
The examiner can help NFIP policyholders recover by being responsive 
to policyholder inquiries, proactively examining reports, promptly issuing 
payments, and providing appropriate and professional communications.

INTRO – 7
FOR MORE INFO

Oversight-13
	
• Involve senior flood insurance claims professionals or reassign claims with 
the policyholder’s best interests in mind, when needed. 
	
• Ensure adjusters on new assignments are contacting policyholders as 
soon as possible, and that communications expectations are properly met.
	
• Validate that preliminary reports, advance payments, referrals for 
underwriting review and engineer assignments, and 30-day status reports 
are sent in a timely manner in compliance with published time standards.
	
• Actively supervise and oversee claims handled by adjusters-in-training. 
	
• Review and ensure accuracy on all claims, in particular assignments for 
less frequent or more complex claim types, such as manufactured homes, 
claims of foundation damage, and all GP-insured and condo-type losses.
	
• Support the review of pending or final claims by NFIP General Adjusters 
(random or at the request of FEMA), policyholders (or representative), or 
NFIP insurers. The adjusting company or adjuster must be able to discuss 
all activity on a claim if the policyholder or representative makes any 
inquiry (e.g., appeal, appraisal, litigation, etc.). 
	
• The adjusting company must have high standards when monitoring 
professional conflicts of interest. 
Examiners
Authority. Examiners administrate claims on behalf of the NFIP insurers. 
Accordingly, they have the authority to pay claims, to make and confirm 
coverage determinations, and to deny coverage when appropriate. Examiners 
should offer guidance to adjusters as needed. Examiners engage with the 
adjuster to guide policyholders through the entire NFIP claims process - 
from the first notice of loss to their final claim determination. By sharing 
and communicating their knowledge of the SFIP to the policyholder, claims 
examiners can help policyholders understand the key actions necessary to 
smooth out the claims journey.
Responsibilities. Claims examiner responsibilities include:
	
• Adjuster assignment, whether done by an automated system or done 
manually, and monitoring performance for timely contact and inspection. 
	
• Managing the claim file, including:
	– Issuing advance payments (when authorized);
	– Resolving underwriting issues with their underwriting department;
	– Settling coverage issues within the claims department; 
	– Overseeing adjusters to ensure timely reporting and settlement 
recommendations; 
	– Affirming and validating adjuster recommendations, as supported by 
evidence in the claim file;
	– Recommending and hiring experts; and
	– Issuing proper and timely payments.
	
• Claims reporting and forms management including:
	– Pivot (NFIP system of record) reporting; and
	– Proper expense payments.

• Communicating with policyholders, including:

FEMA expects every examiner handling NFIP flood losses to:
	
• Understand their first obligation to a policyholder is to identify coverage 
under the SFIP;
	
• Obtain comprehensive reports from adjusters allowing the examiner to 
resolve claims with accuracy and efficiency; 
	
• Understand their payment decisions must be in accordance with the 
SFIP; and
	
• Attend an annual NFIP claims presentation in order to hear FEMA’s 
current messaging on claims.
Code of 
Conduct
Flood insurance claims professionals (i.e., 
independent adjusters, adjusting firms, NFIP 
insurers’ staff adjusters, and examiners), must 
uphold the highest standards of honesty, impartiality, 
integrity, and professional conduct. These principles 
are essential to ensuring the proper performance 
of NFIP-related duties and maintaining the trust and 
confidence of NFIP policyholders. 
Flood insurance claims professionals must conduct 
themselves with courtesy, integrity, and a strong 
sense of responsibility toward policyholders. Flood 
insurance claims professionals must act promptly and diligently in serving 
policyholders, demonstrating professional behavior that reflects positively on 
both the NFIP insurer and the NFIP. 
FEMA does not accept any flood insurance claims professional conflict  
of interest. 
	
• Any flood insurance claims professionals that perform work on behalf of 
an NFIP insurer, or is registered in the Flood Adjuster Capacity Program, 
may not perform any service related to NFIP claims or business (including 
support, inspections, consulting, or estimating) for or as a public adjuster 
(licensed or not), or give representation adverse to the NFIP. 
	
• Adjusters, including adjusting firms, also may not adjust claims for a 
property in which the adjuster or immediate family member owns an 
interest, nor can an adjuster accept any money from a third party to steer 
business to a specific firm or individual. 
	
• Flood insurance claims professionals may not accept monetary or non-
monetary incentives from policyholders. 
	
• The use of the FCN by an adjuster or adjusting firm for any purpose other 
than adjusting a flood insurance claim on behalf of an NFIP insurer is 
improper and may result in immediate suspension or revocation of the 
FCN. FEMA may refer the improper usage of the FCN to investigators as 
necessary to protect the integrity of the NFIP. 
“Ethics is  
knowing the  
difference between 
what you have a  
right to do and  
what is right to do.”
—Justice Potter Stewart

NFIP Claims Manual  |  Introduction

INTRO – 9
Consequences 
of a Code of 
Conduct Violation
FOR MORE INFO
See Protect Policyholder 
Privacy on page Claims 
Journey-4
Investigation 
Procedure of NFIP 
Code of Conduct 
Violations
NFIP insurers, including FEMA’s Direct Servicing Agent, and their adjusting firms 
have an obligation to protect the integrity of the NFIP. When an NFIP insurer 
or adjusting firm becomes aware of an allegation or possible violation of the 
NFIP’s Code of Conduct, they must: 
	
• Conduct an internal investigation and coordinate with FEMA. 
	
• If a violation is confirmed by the NFIP insurer or adjusting firm, they must 
notify FEMA, immediately, and give all supporting documentation, including 
their findings and recommendations. 
FEMA will investigate all complaints asserting a violation of the NFIP’s Code of 
Conduct to confirm the validity of the complaint. 
	
• FEMA will notify the flood insurance claims professional of the allegations 
by email and provide the person, at least, ten (10) business days to 
respond to the allegations, with all pertinent information. 
	
• FEMA will notify the flood insurance claims professional of its findings and 
determination by email, with an opportunity to submit an administrative 
appeal to the Assistant Administrator for Federal Insurance Directorate, 
within twenty (20) calendar days of notification. 
	
• However, if no response to the notice of alleged violation to the NFIP’s 
Code of Conduct is received from the flood insurance claims professional 
within the ten (10) business days period, FEMA may consider its 
investigation complete and notify the flood insurance claims professional 
of FEMA’s final determination, according to its findings, without an 
opportunity for an administrative appeal.
	
• If FEMA determines that there was a violation of the NFIP’s Code of 
Conduct, the flood insurance claims professional must be removed or 
suspended from handling any NFIP claim or business for a period FEMA 
deems necessary to remedy the underlying violation, but in no case less 
than one (1) year. If FEMA determines that an adjuster or adjusting firm 
violated the NFIP’s Code of Conduct and the violation is not amenable to 
remedy, the final determination may include the indefinite or permanent 
revocation of the FCN.
	
• Reinstatement of the flood insurance claims professional, including 
reinstatement of the FCNs, may be requested upon application and 
demonstration of compliance with FEMA’s final determination, and subject 
to FEMA’s approval. 
Documentation can be sent to the NFIP via the following method:
E-mail: NFIPAdjusterMailbox@fema.dhs.gov
If sent over email, you must password-protect, encrypt, or otherwise  
protect the privacy of any personally identifiable information contained  
within the documentation.

<!-- Page 33 -->

MARCH 2025 EDITION	
CLAIMS JOURNEY – 1
NFIP Claims Manual  |  Section One – Claims Journey
This Manual presents the flood insurance claims professional with two  
major categories: 
1.	Customer experience expectations throughout the policyholder  
claims journey, and 
2.	Guidance concerning specific provisions of the Standard Flood Insurance 
Policy (SFIP). 
This first section, Claims Journey, sets out an idealized multi-step process 
that every claim should follow in the interest of completeness and customer 
service. This section addresses what it means to provide good service 
from the adjuster in the field, good service from the independent adjusting 
company, and good service from the NFIP insurer's claims department. 
The next section, SFIP Guidance, provides FEMA’s guidance and interpretations 
for specific provisions of the SFIP. This guidance is organized hierarchically 
to support consistent treatment of policyholders, limit confusion, and ensure 
accurate basis for the denial of claims. It includes entries specific to Increased 
Cost of Compliance (ICC) coverage and special claims handling scenarios. 
Approach to this 
Manual

---
<!-- Page 34 -->

Section One – Claims Journey  |  NFIP Claims Manual
CLAIMS JOURNEY – 2	
MARCH 2025 EDITION
The Claims Journey provides an overview of the major actions that flood 
insurance claims professionals will take, both with and without the policyholder. 
This journey is written with the Dwelling Form in mind but will reference General 
Property (GP) and Residential Condominium Building Association Policy (RCBAP) 
concepts wherever appropriate.
Our customer service requirements permeate every step of the journey, keeping 
with FEMA’s Core Values of Compassion, Fairness, Integrity, and Respect. 
Flood insurance claims professionals must abide by FEMA's Core Values. These 
themes, set out in the table below, will echo throughout the pages that follow.
FEMA CORE VALUES
Compassion
Be empathetic to the stressful circumstances the 
policyholder may be experiencing and your crucial role in 
helping their recovery. Every interaction with the policyholder 
is an opportunity to cultivate a relationship.
Fairness
Strive to achieve principled, well-reasoned, and just outcomes 
in the execution of all claims, and adjust each claim fairly and 
without unnecessary delay, bias, or preference.
Integrity
Integrity is the foundation of all our actions and is central to 
our conduct. Maintain the highest standards of integrity by 
creating a culture of honesty, consistency, and predictability. 
Trust is the earned result of conducting our actions with 
integrity. Failure to adhere to the highest standards reflects 
poorly on the NFIP.
Respect
Treat all policyholders with dignity and respect. This is not 
only important, but it is also their right.

written under the Group Flood Insurance Policy (GFIP) will only be assigned 
to adjusters by NFIP Direct.

<!-- Page 35 -->

MARCH 2025 EDITION	
CLAIMS JOURNEY – 3
NFIP Claims Manual  |  Section One – Claims Journey
Starting with the notice of loss and throughout the claim, the flood insurance 
claims professional should exercise care when handling all personally 
identifiable information (PII). Sensitive PII (SPII), however, requires special 
handling due to the increased risk of harm to an individual if it is compromised. 
The loss or compromise of SPII can result in embarrassment, inconvenience, 
reputational harm, emotional harm, financial loss, unfairness, and, in rare 
cases, a risk to personal safety.
PII is any information that permits the identity of an individual to be directly 
or indirectly inferred, including any other information that is linked or linkable 
to that individual, regardless of whether the individual is a U.S. citizen, legal 
permanent resident, or visitor to the U.S. 
PII examples include:
	
• Name; 
	
• Date and place of birth; 
	
• Mailing address;
	
• Phone number;
	
• ZIP code; and
	
• Vehicle identifiers (including  
license plate).
SPII is personally identifiable information 
which if lost, compromised, or disclosed 
without authorization, could result in 
substantial harm, embarrassment, 
inconvenience, or unfairness to an 
individual. Further, FEMA considers some 
information SPII when coupled with the 
person’s name or other unique identifier (e.g., personal address, telephone 
number) including: full date of birth, authentication information such as 
mother’s maiden name or passwords, and portions of social security numbers. 
SPII examples include: 
	
• Social Security number;
	
• Financial and bank account numbers;
Protect 
Policyholder 
Privacy
	
• Know, apply, and communicate all updates or changes to insurer guidance. 
Adjusters must be familiar with both the general guidance provided by 
FEMA and any company-specific guidance.
	
• Know when they may offer an advance payment to the policyholder 
with an eligible claim and always check for new guidance on advance 
payments; and
	
• Know when to engage outside professional services on adjustments 
(e.g., foundation damage, salvage, elevation disputes/building rating, 
commercial inventory accounting) and, when necessary, seek the 
appropriate authorization in a timely manner.
What is PII?
What is SPII?

<!-- Page 36 -->

Section One – Claims Journey  |  NFIP Claims Manual
CLAIMS JOURNEY – 4	
MARCH 2025 EDITION
	
• Driver’s license number; and 
	
• Biometric identifiers (e.g., fingerprints).
	
• Other data elements such as citizenship or immigration status, medical 
information, ethnicity, religion, sexual orientation, or lifestyle information, 
in conjunction with the identity of an individual (directly or indirectly 
inferred), are also SPII.
Note: SPII requires stricter handling guidelines because of the increased risk 
to an individual if the data is inappropriately accessed or compromised. Some 
categories of PII are sensitive as stand-alone data elements, including Social 
Security numbers and driver’s license or state identification numbers. 
How to 
Protect Privacy
Flood insurance claims professionals should follow basic steps to ensure 
policyholders' PII and SPII (generally, PII) are protected. 
	
• When sharing PII by email, all documents should be password-protected. 
	
• Never email PII to a personal email account to work remotely. Flood 
insurance claims professionals' personally owned computers and other 
devices should not be used to access, save, store, or host sensitive PII.
	
• Do not leave PII unattended on desks, printers, fax machines, or copiers. 
Secure PII in a locked desk drawer, file cabinet, or similar locked enclosure 
when not in use. When using PII, keep it in an area where access is 
controlled and limited to persons with an official need to know. Avoid 
faxing PII, if possible.
	
• Encrypt PII stored on CDs, DVDs, hard drives, USB flash drives, or other 
removable media prior to mailing or sharing.
	
• Do not share account information like usernames or passwords with 
anyone. Do not keep username or password information in a place that is 
accessible to others (e.g., on a sticky note on your computer).
	
• Only store PII on shared drives if access can be restricted to persons 
with proper authorization. Both folders and documents should be 
password protected. 
	
• Do not post PII on the internet (including social media and other 
networking sites) or multi-access calendars that can be accessed by 
individuals who do not have a need to know. 
	
• Government issued identifications (e.g., driver's licenses) should not be 
photographed and placed in the claim file, unless needed. Government 
issued identifications should be redacted to protect PII.
NFIP insurers may have additional guidelines for addressing PII. Flood 
insurance claims professionals should follow the NFIP insurer's direction. 
What is SPII?

CLAIMS JOURNEY – 5
NOTICE  
OF LOSS
The policyholder must promptly notify the NFIP insurer that a loss occurred and 
provide information critical to the investigation of the claim, (see Dwelling and 
General SFIPs, Article VII.G.1; and RCBAP SFIP, Article VIII.G.1). 
	The policyholder’s claim begins with the written notice of loss.
	Flood insurance claims professionals must document the policyholder’s 
reasons for any delay in reporting a loss. It is crucial that the reason(s) 
provided by the policyholder for the delay in reporting the loss is 
thoroughly documented.
 !	 A policyholder’s failure to provide timely notice of loss can be a basis for 
denial of a claim. Proper documentation can assist in determining whether 
the delay was justifiable under the terms of the policy.

Setting 
Expectations with 
the Policyholder
Notice 
of Loss
FEMA expects high-quality claims processing service from NFIP insurers. Flood 
insurance claims professionals must: 
	
• Let the policyholder know what to expect and when to expect it (e.g., when 
the adjuster will call back to make the appointment). 
	
• Follow NFIP insurer pre-inspection advance payment procedures, as 
authorized by FEMA, according to guidance provided in this Manual. 
	
• Provide helpful guidance policyholders can take to help with their recovery, 
for example by suggesting they: 
	– Take as many photographs of the building and personal property 
damages as necessary to properly document the loss; 
	– Do not throw away anything the policyholder wants to claim;
	– Give information on damage tear-out, clean-up, and building dry out, 
especially if the policyholder intends to hire a contractor; 
Actions to Take
The NFIP insurer should gather important policyholder information with the 
notice of loss: loss location, lienholder(s), coverage amount(s), temporary 
address (if any), contact number(s), and email.
	
• Accurately record details of the flood from the policyholder, even if the loss 
is minor.
	
• Include a brief description of the loss, floodwater depth, extent of damage 
(including affected room areas), and any unique circumstances. This 
information will help the adjusting firm to properly prioritize and assign the 
loss. If there is a question about the accuracy of the date of loss, note it.

---
<!-- Page 38 -->

CLAIMS JOURNEY – 6	
MARCH 2025 EDITION
NOTICE  
OF LOSS
Failure to provide a notice of loss may prejudice the ability of the NFIP insurer 
to inspect the loss, identify the cause and extent of damage, and determine 
applicable coverage under the SFIP. When the policyholder delays the notice 
of loss, it is important to determine if the delay harms the NFIP insurer’s 
ability to fully investigate the loss and the claim. Some examples include 
the policyholder reporting the loss after repairs are performed, or when an 
avoidable delay causes damage to undamaged property or changes damaged 
property from salvageable to non-salvageable. 
	– In contrast, a justifiable delay could be due to an order issued by local 
authorities or prolonged inundation which prevents prompt access to 
the insured property. The flood insurance claims professional must 
ask the policyholder questions to understand the circumstances of 
and reasons for the delay and report the reason to the NFIP insurer. 
If the late notification of loss has harmed the NFIP insurer’s ability to 
properly and fully investigate the loss and claim, and the policyholder’s 
explanation does not provide reasonable justification, the NFIP insurer 
has the right to deny the claim.
Below is a non-exhaustive list of actions FEMA suggests flood insurance claims 
professionals take when policyholders submit their notice of loss. 
This list is intended to serve as a guide and should be adapted based on 
specific claim circumstances and NFIP requirements.
	 Review information in the first notice of loss—contact information  
(phone, email), mortgagee/payees, other insureds, mailing address (if 
displaced), etc.
	 Share link to Identifying Your Advocates After a Flood flier: https://agents.
Reporting 
Delays
Best Practices 
Checklist
Initial Actions
	– If able, remove flood-wetted materials such as carpet as soon as 
possible, after they are photographed, to start the dry out process; 
	– Save samples of flooring materials (e.g., carpet) and the like;
	– Separate the damaged and undamaged property;
	– Discard hazardous items like household chemicals and spoiled foods 
as soon as possible after they are photographed; and 
	– Help the policyholder know what to look out for when hiring a 
remediation, drying, or emergency service contractor.
Write Your Own 
Company Rules
When there is a delay in reporting the loss, NFIP insurers may require the use 
of a non-waiver agreement. The non-waiver should include the reason for the 
non-waiver and the policyholder’s explanation for the delay.
The adjuster should have the policyholder sign the non-waiver agreement 
immediately. If the policyholder refuses to sign the non-waiver agreement, 
the insurer may decide to send a Reservation of Rights. The adjuster should 
continue the inspection and review.

	 Set expectations with 
the policyholder for 
how the claim will

Pre-Inspection 
Advance Payments 
(When Authorized)
Policyholder Tips
Advance payments help policyholders start their recovery sooner and reduce 
disaster suffering. Advance payment amounts must be deducted from the final 
claim payment, after a full inspection and adjustment of the loss.
Option 1: $5,000. Upon notice of loss from the policyholder with an active flood 
insurance policy, FEMA may allow a payment on building and contents losses 
combined (less deductibles). The NFIP insurer must obtain verbal or written 
statements from the policyholder and document the following:
	 A flood (as defined at SFIP, Article II.B) directly damaged the insured property;
	 The source of the flood;
	 A brief description of how the flood occurred, with supporting 
documentation from an official weather bureau or reputable news media;
	 An explanation of other flood effects to support that loss is in excess of 
the advanced amount (e.g., flooding on street, neighboring properties);
	 Approximate floodwater depth (interior floor level, exterior);
	 Whether the extent of damage is confined to an area subject to coverage 
limitations or not;
	 A brief description of damage to the building and personal property 
items; and
	 Any prior loss, to avoid duplicated payment for non-repaired  
property items.
Option 2: $20,000. With more substantive documentation to complement all 
the information required by option 1, the NFIP insurer may offer payment, when 
authorized by FEMA, up to $20,000 on building and contents losses combined 
(less deductibles) when they also obtain:
	 Photographs depicting floodwater depths and damage to property; and
	 Verification of any out-of-pocket expenses related to repair or replacement 
of insured property (e.g., receipts, invoices, etc.)
Identifying Your Advocates After a Flood 
Recovering from a flood can feel overwhelming. The National Flood Insurance Program 
(NFIP) is ready to help you recover faster and more fully. In the wake of a flood disaster, you 
should expect multiple individuals to offer assistance, including those from insurance 
companies, nonprofits, government agencies and more.
Fraud After a Flood
Unfortunately, scammers may pose as government agents or damage assessors in the 
aftermath of a flood event. Protect your identity and stay informed by remembering these tips:  
 
•
Officials from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS), FEMA, the U.S. Small 
Business Administration (SBA) and other federal agencies will never charge you for a 
damage assessment or flood insurance adjuster visit.
 
•
Inspectors or adjusters never require Social Security or bank account numbers.
	
• If evacuated, don’t return to the property until it is safe to do so.
	
• Take photographs and videos of the flood damage to the items, especially 
if discarding them. A policyholder should not dispose of non-hazardous or 
non-perishable items prior to an adjuster’s inspection.

• Save swatches and samples of any discarded items (e.g., carpet,

Coverage 
Reminders 
Policyholder 
Representatives
NFIP insurers may not disclose a policyholder’s information to a policyholder’s 
representative or allow a representative to act on behalf of a policyholder 
without obtaining a letter of representation signed by all policyholders named 
on the policy. At minimum, a letter of representation must include:
	
• Policyholder’s full name;
	
• Policyholder’s current address;
	
• Policyholder’s date and place of birth;
	
• Name of third-party representative; and
	
• Statement from policyholder authorizing the authorized representative 
to act on their behalf and for the insurer to release records to the 
representative.
The policyholder’s signature must either be notarized or submitted with the 
following statement prescribed by 28 U.S.C. 1746:
I declare (or certify, verify, or state) under penalty of perjury that the foregoing 
is true and correct. 
Executed on (date). (Signature)

CUSTOMER EXPERIENCE
Prioritizing GP Losses
When dealing with commercial 
losses, it is necessary to inspect 
the property as soon as possible 
after the notice of loss. This 
will benefit the policyholder by 
preserving the ability of the adjuster 
to inspect the damage and help 
control the claim handling.
Policyholder 
Communications
The adjuster must contact the policyholder or their chosen representative 
within 48 hours of receiving the loss assignment. FEMA expects the adjuster 
to show compassion and empathy when discussing the loss and arranging 
the property inspection. 
The adjuster should use the opportunity of the first contact call to establish 
trust with the policyholder. This begins by showing patience with the policyholder 
and actively listening to their concerns before addressing the next steps to their 
claim journey.
Discussion with 
the Policyholder
	 Explain how the policyholder should 
prepare for the inspection;
	 Encourage the policyholder to  
initiate mitigation steps to avoid 
further damage; 
	 Ask them to begin to document their 
loss; and
	 Share other helpful guidance in case 
they need to hire services (e.g., 
electrical, HVAC, damage tear-out, 
drying services).
Actions to Take
	
• Establish a mutually agreed-upon date and time (or window of time) for 
the inspection.
	
• If the date or time of inspection is delayed (e.g., nature of the flood; 
scheduling issues), tell the policyholder how to prepare for the inspection, 
photograph their building and personal property damages, preserve flood 
waterlines, initiate mitigation steps to avoid further damage, start the 
cleanup process, and what to know before hiring any service for damage 
mitigation, drying, or repairs.
	
• A good practice here is to obtain the policyholder’s email address and 
follow up the first contact call with a message confirming the inspection 
date and time, basic instructions towards recovery and the claim process, 
and PDF copies of the NFIP Claims Handbook and ICC Brochure. 
	
• It is best not to discuss coverage limitations (basements or post-FIRM 
elevated buildings) prior to the inspection. Wait until you can confirm facts 
that will be gathered during the inspection.

PAYMENT/POST-CLAIM
Adjusters should be punctual for 
inspections, present themselves in a 
professional manner, and communicate 
any delays. If the adjuster believes the 
inspection will be delayed, they should 
inform the policyholder. Adjusters should 
wear professional attire and present their 
FCN card and any government-issued 
photo ID to the policyholder at the start 
of the inspection. This demonstrates 
professionalism and builds trust.
FEMA expects adjusters to:
	
• Confirm the identity of the person 
at the inspection. Valid government 
issued identifications with the 
person's name and photograph (e.g., 
driver's licenses) must be checked to 
confirm the identity of the person participating in the inspection. It can also 
be used to verify if the insured building is the principal residence if it is the 
policyholder's identification card and also contains a physical address. 
	
• Communicate the coverage and limitations to policyholders during  
the inspection.

Overview
CUSTOMER EXPERIENCE
Consider the Context
When conducting an inspection, 
adjusters should avoid situations 
that could cast them in a bad light or 
inadvertently offend the culture or  
values of the policyholder. 
When minors are present in the 
building that requires inspection, 
the adjuster should never enter 
unless there is an adult present, 
preferably the policyholder or 
policyholder’s representative.
The NFIP requires all losses to be 
assigned to a qualified adjuster with 
a valid FCN. Adjuster professionalism, 
empathy towards the policyholder for 
the loss to their property and potential 
financial ramifications, and meaningful 
communication are key aspects to the 
inspection. This helps to build trust and 
lay the groundwork for a prompt and 
successful claim resolution. The adjuster 
should spend time with the policyholder 
explaining how policy coverage applies to the policyholder’s loss and what is 
necessary for the claim process. The adjuster should give a realistic timeline 
for completion of the estimate.

• Tour the building with the policyholder to view damage and hear concerns.

CUSTOMER EXPERIENCE
Getting the  
Reason Right
Words matter when communicating 
with the policyholder. Be sure to 
distinguish between similar but 
separate concepts. For example, there 
is a difference between a finding of 
no general condition of flood and no 
direct physical loss by or from flood. 
Using the right terminology can build 
trust and avoid unnecessary conflict.
Policyholder 
Interactions
The policyholder is in the best situation to tell the adjuster what is important to 
them and what was damaged as a result of the flood. Accordingly, the adjuster 
should invite the policyholder to 
discuss the loss and have them 
conduct a tour of the property. 
Giving the policyholder the 
opportunity to lead a room-by-room 
tour of the property will allow the 
adjuster to listen and take notes. 
As part of active listening, the 
adjuster should hold off on taking 
photographs until later in the 
inspection. At the end of the tour, 
the adjuster should spend time with 
the policyholder and review what 
was learned to ensure information 
was captured correctly and all 
concerns were understood. 
ADJUSTER TIPS
Documenting No Condition of Flood
When the adjuster cannot find evidence of a 
flood, the claim file must include documentation 
that supports the facts. For example:
	
• Photographs showing the lack of an exterior waterline 
on the building at the lowest adjacent grade; 
	
• Lack of flood-borne debris in the yard; undisturbed 
vegetation in the yard and in the areas immediately 
adjacent to the described location; or
	
• Interview notes obtained from a canvass of neighbors 
and nearby building occupants. 
If the cause of damage to property is understood 
by the adjuster to be other than flood, such as 
water entering through or around a window, door 
threshold, ceiling, pipe, or drain, etc., photographs 
of the source and other related evidence should 
be provided to support the adjuster’s report.
Cause of Loss
On every flood claim, 
the adjuster must 
confirm whether a 
flood, as defined 
at Article II.B of 
the SFIP, occurred 
at the described 
location or in the 
area. The adjuster 
must also confirm 
direct physical loss 
by or from flood. 
If there was a 
flood in the area, 
the adjuster must 
confirm the flood 
was the proximate 
cause of damage 
to insured property. 
This may require the 
adjuster to canvass

---
<!-- Page 44 -->

CLAIMS JOURNEY – 12	
MARCH 2025 EDITION
INSPECTION
Whenever a building floods, it has a waterline. A waterline is usually evidenced 
by flood debris covering the wall from grade-level or floor slab to the water limit. 
Proper documentation of the waterline is critical to determining the extent and 
scope of damage, but a missing or unclear photographic record of the waterline 
makes it difficult or impossible to support that a flood occurred or to validate 
the scope of loss. Improper documentation can call into question the entire 
adjustment or payment recommendation. Therefore, adjusters must take extra 
time to locate and properly measure, photograph, and document waterlines. 
	
• Record the highest exterior waterline on the building. It is always located 
at the lowest adjacent grade (LAG). 
	
• Recording the minimum exterior waterline, located at the highest adjacent 
grade (HAG), may be helpful on certain losses.
	
• Photograph the full height of the measured waterline from the ground to 
the top of the waterline. 
	
• Take several measured interior waterlines, erring on the side of having 
more documentation of the waterline than less. If the building has more 
than one floor, there should be photos that correspond to each level, 
i.e., measurements for the basement, main floor, a sunken living room, 
attached garage, etc. 
	
• If the adjuster cannot find a waterline, the adjuster should confirm that 
they thoroughly sought out elusive waterlines before documenting the lack 
of a waterline. The adjuster might consider:
	– On the exterior: Behind an outdoor HVAC unit, around support posts, 
underneath a porch or deck.
	– In a crawlspace: Interior sides of foundation walls, piers, floor joists, 
or plumbing lines. Note that water damage to the insulated main 
supply duct is not always an indicator of flood inundation, but may 
instead be attributable to long-term exposure to condensation from 
under-insulation or a leak in the supply trunk line.
	– On the interior: Inside a storm door on the exterior door threshold, or 
on the unfinished MDF backside of particleboard furniture, furniture 
legs, etc. 
the nearby properties for observations from neighbors that describe the events 
in the area or at their location. When documenting a flood that is in question, 
the adjuster should search the local weather bureau for past storm or flood 
forecasts, warning bulletins, or other posted public statements. A search of 
the internet for related articles from local news outlets or publicly posted social 
media may help to support the adjuster’s recommendation. For some events, 
contacting the nearest police or fire department may be needed to help to 
confirm a flood. 
The adjuster must document:
	
• When the flooding began, when the flood receded, and the source of the 
water; and
	
• Whether the flood caused direct physical loss to insured property.
Documenting and 
Measuring the 
Waterline

Assessing Mold 
and Moisture 
Damage Above the 
Waterline
During the inspection, the adjuster should document as much relevant 
information as possible to help with additional claim adjustment and 
examination. This information includes but is not limited to:
	
• An assessment of all property damage; 
	
• Evidence relevant to the recommendation to repair or replace  
damaged property;
	
• Notes with details describing building and content loss items; 
	
• The quantity and quality of damaged property; and
	
• A listing of all machinery and equipment, with serial numbers.
During the inspection, it is important for the adjuster to determine if any 
damages above the waterline were caused by moisture and mold due to the 
policyholder’s failure to remove flood-wetted property items in a timely fashion 
or from rain or wind-driven rainwater, versus whether or not any of this damage 
was caused directly by the length of time floodwater remained inside the 
building or from prolonged exposure of flood-wetted material after floodwaters 
receded due to lack of access to the building beyond the policyholder’s control.
In view of this, the adjuster can gain a better understanding here by noting the 
timespan between the following specific dates:
	
• Date of loss;
Damage 
Assessment
▲ Example of a good photograph: clear picture of the flood debris 
showing the waterline, from the floor slab to the water limit, with 
measurement.
▲ Example of a questionable photograph: 
no flood debris, would not support a 
recommendation of damage by or from flood.

• Date the building became accessible 
once floodwaters receded;
	
• Date the policyholder reported their 
loss;
	
• Date of inspection;
	
• Date of interior damages removal and 
salvageable materials cleaned and 
treated;
	
• Date the building electrical and HVAC 
building systems were restored; and
	
• Date structural drying began.
The adjuster should document the reason for the passage of time in the claim 
file or obtain a signed statement from the policyholder to help support the 
claim. If the amount of time is believed to be unreasonable, the adjuster must 
determine if the delay contributed to the damage above the waterline. 
Because any policyholder’s claim is at risk of incurring non-covered damage 
above the waterline, the insurer and the adjuster should approach this issue 
by addressing its potential to the policyholder at the earliest opportunity during 
the claim journey: at the first notice of loss, first contact call by the adjuster, 
or at the loss inspection. For a specific example of this issue, see FEMA Flood 
Insurance Appeal Decision #C36 in response to a policyholder’s appeal.
Handling a 
Claim with Multiple 
Perils
The SFIP is a single-peril policy and will only pay for direct physical loss by 
or from flood to the insured building and personal property. When a claim 
assignment or loss inspection reveals the involvement of flood damage and 
damage from another peril, information about 
other insurance coverage from the policyholder is 
needed to complete the claim. Additionally, the 
adjuster will have to spend additional time when 
assessing and documenting the damages from 
flood and the other peril.
Handling a flood claim with another peril often 
arises from a tropical weather system or a 
nor’easter. However, losses with flood and 
another peril are not limited to these types of 
events. In some states, insurance regulations 
require homeowners policies that cover fire to 
also cover damage caused by flood or mudflow 
when the flood or mudflow is considered 
connected to the fire event, even if the same 
policy has a coverage exclusion for flood or 
mudflow. Another common example is when a sewer backup occurs from 
a flood in the proximate area, damaging property inside a basement. The 
homeowner may have a rider on their homeowners policy that can cover the 
same loss. Before any claim recommendation is given to the policyholder 
or NFIP insurer, FEMA requires the adjuster to understand the full scope of 
ADJUSTER TIP
Attributing Damage
The terms “peril” and “other 
cause of loss” are not 
interchangeable. A peril is a 
separate cause of damage that 
occurs from the same event 
causing the flood, while other 
cause of loss is a broader term 
describing causes of damage 
that occur separately before 
or after the flood event.

---
<!-- Page 48 -->

CLAIMS JOURNEY – 16	
MARCH 2025 EDITION
INSPECTION
damage, the division of damage between perils, and the potential payment 
implications involving other insurance coverage. 
What To Do at the Inspection
	
• At the outset, if the adjuster determines at least one other peril is involved, 
the adjuster must separate the activity to be performed on the loss 
assignment into two parts: one for flood, and a second for the other peril. 
	
• The adjuster should perform all required inspection activities as normal, as 
if flood is the only cause of damage involved. This includes documenting 
a condition of flood and photographing evidence of exterior and interior 
flood waterlines. Confirm the full scope of flood damage with plenty of 
photographs. For example, if the height of interior floodwater is 25 inches, 
show this waterline in photographs with respect to a doorknob, kitchen 
countertop, desktop computer, tabletop television, garage disposal, toilet 
seat, and the like. This helps to document what installed property items 
are just below or just above the 25-inch interior flood waterline. Apply 
the same thinking with photographing exterior building damage and the 
exterior waterline measurement. 
	
• The adjuster should document the damages caused by the other peril, 
including:
	– Damaged property below the waterline not characteristic of flood 
damage, such as wall framing structurally damaged by wind.
	– Damaged property above the exterior and interior waterline such 
as from rainwater, wind, moisture, mold, or other non-flood perils. 
Photographs of damages from wind and wind-driven rain, noting the 
difference between wind-driven debris and flood-borne debris, and the 
directional orientation of wind damage versus the direction of flood’s 
velocity flow or wave action should be recorded.
	– Fire is easy to document in photographs, but damage from smoke 
is not. Whenever smoke damage is involved, the importance 
of obtaining information about other insurance coverage and 
coordinating and confirming the scope of damage with the other 
insurance adjuster(s) increases.
	– The extent of damage should be noted with greater detail where the 
scope of the other peril is close to, adjacent to, or overlaps with the 
scope of flood damage.
	– The adjuster must thoroughly document the full scope of damages with 
photographs so that the policyholder and the examiner have sufficient 
information to reasonably understand the efforts to properly attribute 
the scopes of loss between flood and the other peril.
	
• During the damage assessment part of the inspection, it is important 
for the adjuster to decipher what damages above the waterline were 
caused by moisture and mold due to the policyholder’s failure to remove 
flood-wetted property items in a timely fashion versus rain or wind-driven 
rainwater, or whether or not the damage was caused directly by or from 
flood due to the length of time floodwater remained inside the building, or 
from prolonged exposure beyond the policyholder’s control.
Handling a 
Claim with Multiple 
Perils
continued

<!-- Page 49 -->

MARCH 2025 EDITION	
CLAIMS JOURNEY – 17
INSPECTION
	
• Once the adjuster has completed the damage assessment and obtained 
the photographs necessary to fully document the claim of covered damage 
and scope of non-covered damage, the adjuster should discuss the next 
steps with the policyholder. This includes discussing the overall scope 
of flood damage and scope of damage from other perils, including other 
causes not associated with the date of loss. 
	
• In addition to the normal discussion points, the adjuster should request 
the following information regarding other insurance that may also covers 
the loss: 
	– Name of other insurance carrier; 
	– Policy number; 
	– Claim number; 
	– Name of any other adjuster; 
	– Phone number and email address; and 
	– Agent’s contact info.
What to do Before Sending the Preliminary Report (See Reporting, 
Preliminary Report for more details on Preliminary Reports) 
In addition to regular flood adjustment responsibilities, the adjuster should 
include in the Preliminary Report:
	
• Sufficient photographs, properly annotated, that describe the risk, 
address any underwriting concerns, and support the cause of loss and 
extent of damage to building and contents in the preliminary assessment. 
For photos of the exterior, the adjuster should make sure the annotations 
include a description of the location depicted in the photos, (e.g., “north 
side, SW corner, or rear crawlspace”). 
	
• If other insurance or another adjuster is indicated, request their 
contact information.
	
• If not already obtained, or if no information has been provided, a copy of 
the adjuster's email to the policyholder requesting the other insurance 
company's coverage and agent and company contact information. 
	
• If deciphering the extent of flood damage and the potential overlap with 
the other peril is problematic or challenging, send a copy of the completed 
request to the NFIP insurer for a forensic damage assessment from a 
state licensed engineer, concisely explaining the issue justifying the 
request (see Reporting, Use of Professional Services). The request should 
include a copy of the Preliminary Report with all photographs, including any 
policyholder-provided photographs with the engineer’s inspection request.
What to do to Complete the Flood Insurance Estimate
	
• If the scope of flood damage does not overlap or border damage 
from the other peril, and these facts are well-documented in the 
flood adjuster’s photographs, there is no need to coordinate the 
claim with the other adjuster. 
Handling a 
Claim with 
Multiple Perils
continued

---
<!-- Page 50 -->

CLAIMS JOURNEY – 18	
MARCH 2025 EDITION
INSPECTION
Example: flood inundates the interior building to two feet, creating a 
four-foot scope of damage with wall painting to the ceilings. Wind also 
damages the roof and nothing more. The scope of damage for both 
perils (flood and wind) do not overlap and do not border the other. The 
adjuster addresses these facts in the “other insurance” section of the 
report, and handles the claim to its conclusion, as normal.
	
• If the scope of flood damage overlaps or borders the scope from the other 
peril, the flood adjuster should make contact with the other adjuster to 
confirm the extent of damage from the other peril. 
Example: flood inundates the interior building to two feet, creating a 
four-foot scope of damage with wall painting to the ceilings. Wind also 
damages the roof, which causes rainwater to damage the drywalled 
ceiling, requiring replacement. The scope of damage for both perils 
(flood and wind) border the other. To ensure the other insurance claim 
does not also paint the walls, since the ceiling drywall replacement 
affects the painted seam at the wall and ceiling, the flood adjuster 
should contact the other adjuster to ensure no overlapping coverage. 
If there is agreement of scope of damage between the adjusters, the flood 
adjuster must document the call in the claim file notes and agree with the 
other adjuster that whoever completes their respective insurance estimate 
first will send the same to the other and confirm the agreed scope. 
	
• If there is disagreement, the flood adjuster should request to exchange 
photos with the other adjuster to better understand the scope and cause 
of damage. If the disagreement continues, the flood adjuster should note 
the scope in question and contact the adjusting firm supervisor to see if 
the other adjuster’s scope is correct, or if a forensic damage assessment 
by a state licensed engineer is needed. When the scope in question 
is resolved with the appropriate documentation, the flood adjuster 
should obtain a copy of the other adjuster’s insurance estimate and any 
supporting engineering report and complete the claim accordingly. 
If the other insurance estimate is not complete by the time the flood 
estimate can be finalized, the flood adjuster should complete the flood 
estimate and zero-out the price of any overlapping line items in the estimate 
that the flood adjuster understands or has been informed is required to 
be covered on the other insurance estimate, or which is supported by the 
engineer's forensic damage assessment. The flood adjuster should then 
send an email with the flood insurance estimate to the other adjuster 
explaining the scope of damage and include a copy of the engineer’s report. 
	
• If the combined flood and other peril repair estimates exceed the value 
of the building (see detailed building valuation), or if the building is non-
repairable (e.g., slab-only remaining or washed-away building), then the 
building is considered a total loss and the claim payment must be based 
on a detailed building valuation, less the scope of damage from the other 
peril, the applicable depreciation, and the policy deductible. If the net value 
exceeds the policy limit, then the claim payment will be the policy limit. 
Handling a 
Claim with Multiple 
Perils
continued

<!-- Page 51 -->

MARCH 2025 EDITION	
CLAIMS JOURNEY – 19
INSPECTION
What to do When Completing the Recommendation to the Policyholder
	
• The flood adjuster should record notes in the insurance estimate 
describing the scope of flood damages versus where the scope of 
damages from the other peril begins. 
	
• If the flood adjuster received the estimate from the other adjuster, the flood 
adjuster should indicate it in the estimate and confirm the flood scope 
versus the scope of damage covered by the other insurance coverage.
	
• On certain claim assignments, the flood adjuster may find it more useful 
to list the full scope of flood damage and the full scope of damage from 
the other peril, while “zeroing out” the unit price for the line items for the 
other peril. Such an effort may help resolve or avoid challenging issues 
or assist with understanding the coverage application of the SFIP on the 
flood insurance claim. 
	
• The flood adjuster should document the estimate to the policyholder in a 
way that is not overwhelming and answers potential questions that can 
reasonably be anticipated before they are asked.
	
• If the SFIP Other Insurance provision (Dwelling and General SFIPs, Article 
VII.B; and RCBAP SFIP, Article VIII.B) requires a proportional distribution 
of the loss with the other insurance coverage, the flood adjuster must 
complete the math calculations on a separate document page and provide 
it to the policyholder with the insurance estimate. When completing the 
information regarding the other insurance calculations, the flood adjuster 
should make sure all math functions are within a single arithmetic 
expression on one side of the equal sign, to avoid a math error. 
Example: When a basement is inundated by sewer backup, where flood 
is in the immediate area and is the proximate cause, the SFIP pays with 
limited coverage and the other insurance policy pays with full coverage up 
to the sewer backup limit (must confirm with the declarations page of the 
other insurance policy). The flood adjuster must prorate the claim based 
on the total amount of insurance coverage involved (flood Coverage A 
amount, plus the other insurance sublimit coverage amount), noting that 
the SFIP is primary up to the other insurance deductible.
Flood policy Coverage A: $200,000 / $2,000 deductible
Other insurance sewer backup sublimit: $30,000 / $1,000 deductible
Scope of covered damage under SFIP = $40,000
The proportion of flood insurance coverage to the total amount of 
insurance coverage involved, multiplied by the amount of SFIP covered 
damage, less the other deductible, plus the amount of the other 
insurance deductible, less the SFIP deductible, equals the payable SFIP 
claim before any non-recoverable depreciation: 
200,000 ÷ (200,000 + 30,000) × (40,000 – 1,000) + 1,000 – 2,000 =  
$32,913.04 SFIP covered RCV payable amount
Based on the above, start with the math functions within the parentheses 
first, then complete the math expression in order from left to right.
Handling a 
Claim with 
Multiple Perils
continued

---
<!-- Page 52 -->

CLAIMS JOURNEY – 20	
MARCH 2025 EDITION
INSPECTION
In this example, the net payable for the covered SFIP claim is $32,913.04 
before any non-recoverable depreciation. The other insurance should cover 
the difference, plus the value of property items not covered in a basement 
under the SFIP, less the other insurance deductible, up to the sewer 
backup sublimit.
Special adjustment considerations when the other peril policy pays 
without a detailed estimate or consideration of multi-peril involvement.
To evaluate a reasonable settlement under the SFIP, it is important to consider 
that the other peril policy payment may include items not covered by the SFIP, 
such as pool decks, decks, elevators, carports, patios, patio covers, and sheds. 
The other peril policy may also cover code compliance or provide increased 
payments for a total loss. The flood adjuster should help the policyholder:
	
• Establish a pre-loss property value based on general contractor pricing to 
rebuild and replace (with like kind and quality) the SFIP-insured building, 
other structures, and equipment existing at the time of loss. 
	
• Identify the quality of finishes and equipment within each structure at 
the time of loss. A subject matter expert (appraiser) may be required 
for an itemized, detailed valuation of all buildings, structures, and 
building equipment. 
	
• If necessary, after request to and approval by the NFIP insurer for a forensic 
damage assessment, the flood adjuster should use a subject matter expert 
(engineer) to determine the cause of loss and apportion the damage to 
each involved peril (see Reporting, Use of Professional Services).
Using the information provided by the subject matter experts, the flood 
adjuster should evaluate the information to recommend if payment is owed by 
the NFIP insurer or if the other peril insurance settlement paid the policyholder 
the pre-loss value of the SFIP-covered property. The flood adjuster should 
assist the policyholder with:
	
• Identifying what was included in the other peril insurance settlement by 
reviewing the other peril insurance policy declarations page to identify 
the building, other structures, personal property, and loss of use, or a 
combination of these. 
	
• Deducting the values of property not covered by the SFIP from the 
other peril insurance settlement (e.g., the SFIP does not cover pools or 
pool decking). 
	
• Listing the property covered under the SFIP and the other peril insurance. 
	
• Identifying items already paid for under the other peril insurance.
	
• Avoiding duplicate claims for the same items under multiple policies for 
multiple perils. 
If payment is owed under the SFIP, the flood adjuster must apply non-
recoverable depreciation and the applicable deductible to determine the 
amount payable under the SFIP claim, subject to coverage limits.
The above list is illustrative and not exclusive because different and 
unexpected issues can arise in claims, and it is not possible to include every 
situation. Proactive handling and effective timely communication of these

complex claims will help ensure that policyholders receive the corresponding

What to do When Completing the 
Closing Report
	
• The narrative report should include 
a section that discusses Other 
Insurance Coverage and include 
a general description of the 
scope, details, and other adjuster 
contact info. If the claim requires 
a proportional loss distribution, 
make sure to include a copy of the 
other insurance calculations.
	
• Include all copies of emails sent or 
received regarding other insurance 
from the agent, other adjuster, or 
the policyholder, including the other insurance estimate.
	
• Additional photos must be properly annotated and submitted with the 
interim report or final report, whichever comes first. 
ADJUSTER TIP
Expect Questions
Just like with the policyholder 
recommendation, the adjuster should 
approach the closing report and 
narrative with the idea to answer 
questions before they are asked. This 
builds trust, avoids phone calls, and 
prevents claim processing delays.
The SFIP will not pay for damage caused by a flood that was a continuation of 
a flood that existed prior to coverage becoming effective. Also, the SFIP will 
not pay for damage caused by a flood that was a continuation of a flood that 
existed on or before the day the application and payment for coverage were 
submitted to the NFIP insurer.
	
• In other words, the SFIP does not insure damage from a flood that began 
before a new policy’s waiting period began or has run (or coverage, if no 
waiting period was in effect) 
even if the flood did not damage 
the insured property until after 
the waiting period ended (or 
coverage was in effect) i.e., if 
"a flood was in progress." If a 
flood was already in progress 
before a policyholder requested 
an increase in coverage on an 
existing policy, the SFIP will only 
pay for damage that occurred 
to the insured property from 
the same flood event after the 
waiting period (or increased 
coverage, if no waiting period) 
has run, to the lesser policy 
limits.
ADJUSTER TIP
Flood Data Sources
Data available to assist in determining 
the time of a flood occurrence include:
	
• The National Oceanic and 
Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) 
for data on river and lake levels;
	
• News reports and social media or 
other sources that may help an 
adjuster to determine when flooding 
was first reported; and
	
• Community officials for assistance 
in determining lake or river levels.

CLAIMS JOURNEY – 22

INSPECTION
	
• When a flood claim is reported, the insurer must determine when the 
flood started and if the flood-in-progress exclusion applies. The adjuster 
must investigate if a flood is already in progress on or before the date the 
policyholder submitted the application for coverage under the SFIP and the 
correct premium paid.
	
• The adjuster should interview the policyholder and conduct a neighborhood 
canvass to determine the location of the floodwaters in relation to the 
insured property location at the time the policy became effective. In the 
narrative, the adjuster must detail their process and evidence used for 
recommending coverage or denial under the SFIP.
Whether a flood is in progress for a claim is evaluated on an individual basis. 
FEMA will apply the V.B. Flood in Progress exclusion in the SFIP regardless of 
individual property, city, county, or parish boundaries. FEMA does not interpret 
the V.B. exclusion as triggered only when floodwaters physically touch the 
insured building.
Flood-in-Progress Adjustment Tips. 
Evidence that a flood may be in 
progress on or before the date the 
policyholder submitted the application 
for coverage or request for additional 
coverage under the SFIP and the 
correct premium may include:
	
• A flood in the area where the 
insured building is located 
caused by the same source of 
flooding as the flood on the 
insured property; or
	
• An event initiating a flood that 
causes damage, such as: 
a spillway opening, a levee 
breaching, a dam releasing 
water, or water escaping from 
the banks of a waterway 
(stream, river, creek, etc.).
When a loss occurs on or before 
the date the waiting period for coverage 
began or when there is evidence that 
a flood may have been in progress 
on or before the date the policyholder 
submitted a request for additional coverage under the SFIP and the correct 
premium, the adjuster should identify and investigate the following:
	
• The date the policy was purchased;
	
• The date the policy or additional coverage went into effect;
	
• The date the flooding as defined by the policy at II.B began;
	
• Evidence of flood prevention prior to the effective date of the policy (e.g., 
temporary levees, sandbagging, constructed berms, pumps); and

If the adjuster needs assistance in confirming when a flood began, the adjuster

Building Scope
The scope of damage includes room measurements, preliminary damage 
findings, and photographs that document and describe the quantity, quality, 
and extent of damage to insured property. The adjuster should account for 
conditions unique to the loss or location and factors such as exceptional 
quantities, craftsmanship, or assemblies, which may require an adjustment 
to the scope and price to repair. Adjustments of this nature should be 
documented by the adjuster with a notation in the estimate under the 
applicable line item. 
The adjuster must submit an itemized listing of all damaged property items, 
organized room-by-room, in the unit-cost style of estimating. The unit pricing 
should include all costs related to labor, material, and equipment usage, and 
only include the expense which restores the property with like kind and quality 
material and labor. Pricing should be reasonable and customary to the loss 
and location. 
Note: It is important for the adjuster and policyholder to identify damaged 
items that are salvageable and require cleaning, refinishing, or repair; and the 
damages that are non-salvageable and require replacement. 
Important: The adjuster must consistently document all state and local sales 
tax on applicable goods or services, subject to applicable depreciation. 
The adjuster must inform the policyholder that once they obtain a signed 
agreement with a contractor, if differences exist with the insurance estimate, it 
may be necessary for the adjuster, supervising adjuster, or the claim examiner 
to communicate back with the policyholder and the contractor to understand 
the pricing differences or explain coverage issues to reach a claim settlement. 
Personal Property 
(Contents)
FEMA expects the adjuster to list, 
document, and value the entire 
contents loss and give the list to 
the policyholder for review. If the 
policyholder elects to complete the 
inventory, the adjuster is expected to 
start the inventory and ensure that 
the policyholder understands how to 
document the remaining contents.
The adjuster must include documentation to support the claimed loss by 
photographing the extent of damages, quantities, qualities, and value. The 
adjuster must ensure coverage and depreciation are appropriately applied. 
Adjusters must also explain their judgment regarding repair or replacement 
when it may not be apparent to the policyholder or the examiner. The 
contents inventory should include notations specific to the applicable line

Payment/ 
Post-Claim
All flood insurance claims professionals, including the adjuster, must 
understand that after a flood loss, the policyholder is experiencing a 
challenging time in their life. This experience is not the same for every 
policyholder. Policyholders may experience 
different levels of stress from the flood event at 
different stages during their recovery process. 
The plight and recovery of every policyholder 
is our primary focus, and FEMA relies on the 
patience and empathy of adjusters to create 
the best claim journey for policyholders. To this 
end, FEMA recommends the following:
	
• Offer written materials. Give a copy of the: 
	– NFIP Claims Handbook, and 
	– ICC Brochure.
	
• Explain coverage. Spend time reviewing the SFIP with the policyholder, 
showing them the policy language or explaining how certain provisions 
or terms apply to their claim. This includes discussing both coverage 
and non-coverage issues. Inform the policyholder about the adjuster’s 
role in helping the policyholder document their claim and make a 
recommendation to them and the insurer, explaining and showing support 
for that recommendation. The 
SFIP does not provide adjusters 
any authority to approve or deny 
claims or to tell the policyholder 
what the NFIP insurer will pay. 
	
• Explain the claim process and 
set expectations. Inform the 
policyholder of the general steps 
in the claim process and how 
long it will take. When can the 
policyholder expect an advance 
payment (if requested)? When 
will they receive the adjuster’s 
recommendation? When will 
the NFIP insurer make a claim 
decision and any payment?

they work with.

Post-Inspection 
Advance Payments 
(When Authorized)
In a large flooding event, FEMA will generally authorize advance payments 
to policyholders via a bulletin. When authorized by FEMA, requested by the 
policyholder, and recommended by the adjuster, the NFIP insurer may issue 
post-inspection advance payments. The adjuster must verify the mailing 
address so the NFIP insurer can send the payment to the correct location. 
Option 1: 25 Percent Advance. NFIP insurers may issue an advance payment 
of up to 25 percent of the reserve amount indicated on the Preliminary Report 
for each coverage type (i.e., building, contents), less deductible(s). The adjuster 
must provide the NFIP insurer with the Preliminary Report and any other 
applicable documentation normally submitted or required with the Preliminary 
Report. This includes, but is not limited to:
	 Photographs;
	 An Advance Payment Request form signed by the policyholder;
	 Any underwriting memoranda;
	 An Adjuster’s Preliminary Damage Assessment; and
	 An adjuster narrative addressing any prior NFIP paid loss.
Option 2: 50 Percent Advance. For building coverage only. The NFIP insurer 
may issue a larger advance payment amount of up to 50 percent of the 
reserve amount indicated on the Preliminary Report for building coverage, less 
deductible. For this type of post-inspection advance payment, in addition to 
obtaining and verifying the documentation listed in the preceding Option 1, the 
NFIP insurer should also obtain more substantive documentation, including a 
signed contract between the policyholder and the contractor, along with the 
estimate of repair. The estimate should itemize the repair and costs to the 
insured property.
Option 3: Building Valuation Loss Assessment (BVLA). The insurer may issue 
a post-inspection advance payment based on the FEMA-authorized BVLA 
advance payment method. In addition to the Preliminary Report and any other 
applicable documentation that is normally submitted or required, the adjuster 
should include the following:
	 The proper photographs;
	 An Advance Payment Request form signed by the policyholder,
	 Any underwriting memoranda;
	 An Adjuster’s Preliminary Damage Assessment; and
	 An adjuster narrative addressing any prior NFIP paid loss.
	 The adjuster must also submit a properly completed BVLA worksheet, 
which is issued by FEMA following major flooding events, to the insurer.

<!-- Page 59 -->

MARCH 2025 EDITION	
CLAIMS JOURNEY – 27
DISCUSSION
Reporting
Writing the

Rec. CX
Final Adjuster
